      PARAMANU : A FAMILY OF N OVEL E FFICIENT G ENER -
002   ATIVE F OUNDATION L ANGUAGE M ODELS FOR I NDIAN
003
004   L ANGUAGES
005

       Anonymous authors

       Paper under double-blind review
008
009
010
011                                                     A BSTRACT
012
013                We present PARAMANU (which means “atom” in multiple Indian languages), a
014
                   family of novel language models for Indian languages. It is a collection of auto-
                   regressive monolingual, bilingual, and multilingual Indian language models pre-
015
                   trained from scratch, currently covering 10 Indian languages (Assamese, Bangla,
016
                   Hindi, Konkani, Maithili, Marathi, Odia, Sanskrit, Tamil, Telugu) across 5 scripts
017                (Bangla, Devanagari, Odia, Tamil, Telugu). The models are pretrained with a con-
018                text size of 1024 on a single GPU, and are of varying sizes ranging from 13.29 M
019                to 367.5 M parameters. We proposed a RoPE embedding scaling method that
020                enables us to pretrain language models from scratch at larger sequence length con-
021                text size on single GPU without increased GPU memory. than the equivalent GPU
022                memory. We have also developed an efﬁcient and advanced novel tokenizer with
023                least fertility score among existing LLMs for Indian languages using a combina-
024                tion of BPE and Unigram that can also tokenize unseen languages written in the
025
                   same script or the Roman script. We also proposed language speciﬁc tokeniza-
                   tion for multilingual models and domain speciﬁc tokenization for monolingual
026
                   language models. In order to avoid the “curse of multi-linguality” in our multi-
027
                   lingual M PARAMANU model, we pretrained on comparable corpora by typologi-
028                cal grouping using the same script. We proposed and performed pretraining for
029                more than 1 epoch of training for most of our language models. From our re-
030                sults, we observed the language transfer phenomenon from low resource to high
031                resource within languages of the same script and typology. We performed human
032                evaluation of our pretrained models for open end text generation on grammar, co-
033                herence, creativity, and factuality metrics for several languages. Our Paramanu
034                models outperformed standard and multilingual large language models (LLMs)
035                by a large margin in performance despite being smaller in size by 64 to 20 times.
036
                   We studied the impact of language speciﬁc tokenization versus language agnostic
                   tokenization for bilingual language modeling. We also studied the impact of BPE
037
                   versus Unigram tokenization for Devanagari script languages. We further created
038
                   instruction-tuning datasets and instruction-tuned our pretrained models on 23,000
039                instructions in respective languages except Hindi, for which we used 75,000 in-
040                structions. Comparison with multilingual LLMs on various commonsense reason-
041                ing benchmarks for natural language understanding, natural language inference,
042                and machine reading comprehension shows the advantage of our models. The
043                performance of our Paramanu models leads to the conclusion that high quality
044                generative language models are possible without high amount of compute power
045                (FLOPS) and enormous number of parameters.
046
047
048
      1       I NTRODUCTION
049
      World’s new age growth arenas are non-English speakers from countries such as India, China, In-
050
      donesia, Mexico, South Africa, etc., where more people will connect to the Internet for information
051   need without language acting as boundaries. Multi-lingualism represents freedom of expression
052   and diversity in a country like India. According to a Guardian article1 by Andras Kornai, “95% of
053
          1
              http://labs.theguardian.com/digital-language-divide/


                                                               1
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


054
      all languages in use today will never gain traction online”; this highlighting the digital language
055   divide. Despite around 7,000 languages, current NLP technologies cover only 12%, leaving many
056   non-English and non-European languages underserved. This, in our personal opinion, is an ex-
057   tremely incapacitating bias and language divide for the global digital world where the non-English,
058   non-European language world has been left out. According to the Indian Census 2011, there are 22
059   ofﬁcial languages and more than hundred others with a sizeable number of speakers in India. Both
060   Hindi and Bangla (Bengali), despite being the world’s 5th and 6th most spoken languages respec-
061   tively according to Babel2 are still underrepresented in today’s NLP technology. Multilingual NLP
062   faces the challenges of having lack of quality benchmark datasets covering diverse languages from
063
      different language families and especially under-represented languages, and is typically referred as
      “low-resource” in the NLP community. Large language models (LLMs) such as GPTNeoX (Black
064
      et al., 2022), OPT (Zhang et al., 2022), LLaMa (Touvron et al., 2023), PaLM (Chowdhery et al.,
065
      2023), GPT-2-XL (Radford et al., 2019), GPT-J (Wang & Komatsuzaki, 2021), etc. have primarily
066   focused on English and mostly European languages whereas other languages have not been given
067   priority. Bloom (Workshop et al., 2023) is considered to be the biggest multilingual auto-regressive
068   model that has been built till now; it has been pretrained on 45 languages including Indian lan-
069   guages. However, Indian languages are morphologically richer and typologically distinct than lan-
070   guages written in the Latin script and, hence, grouping them together without considering linguistics
071   nuances often leads to poor performance of LLMs for low resource languages.
072   This work is an attempt to make language technology more accessible for Indian languages. In
073   this work, we focus on 10 Indian languages (Assamese, Bangla/Bengali, Hindi, Konkani, Maithili,
074   Marathi, Odia, Sanskrit, Tamil, Telugu) written in 5 distinct scripts (Assamese-Bengali, Devanagari,
075   Odia, Tamil, Telugu) comprising of more than billion speakers in the global world. Our goal is
076   to show that generative language models for low-resource Indian languages can be trained from
077   scratch with limited compute and token budget, without using English corpora. We excluded English
078   due to its linguistic differences with Indian languages in terms of typology, script, morphology
079   and grammar, aiming to maintain language purity and typology grouping. Additionally, existing
080
      multilingual models often have an English-centric bias due to the large imbalance in data. To the
      best of our knowledge, the existing LLMs and multilingual LLMs struggle to generate grammatically
081
      correct and coherent sentences in Indian languages (and reasoning ability is too far) as shown in
082
      Appendix Tables 20, 26, 27, 28, 29, 30, 30, 32, 33, 35, 36, 37, 38, 39, 40. Many LLMs even
083   generate text in even Arabic (Table 29) or Japanese (Table 39) scripts when prompted with prompts
084   in Bengali, Hindi, and Sanskrit. We also found that LLMs including ChatGPT (Oct’23) were not
085   able to distinguish languages of the same script such as Bengali versus Assamese. Human evaluation
086   by annotators conﬁrm this (Section A.5 in Appendix). Our models, based on Transformer decoders
087   (Vaswani et al., 2017), are enhanced with improvements in the architecture. These enhancements,
088   improvements and novelties make our models efﬁcient, small but strong. They have been pretrained
089   from scratch to support context size of 1024 without requiring higher physical memory on a single
090   NVIDIA A100-PCIE-40GB GPU. Our model architecture has the ability to capture a much higher
091
      context size without requiring equivalent physical memory; in contrast, the LLaMa-1 model was
      pretrained from scratch using 2048 GPUs for a context size of 2048. Our models are of varying
092
      sizes and of three types: (1) monolingual language models, (2) bilingual language models, and
093
      (3) multilingual language models. Both bilingual and multilingual language models are pretrained
094   from scratch on comparable corpora and with typological grouping of languages to avoid curse of
095   multilinguality.
096
097
      We summarize our contributions as follows.
098
              1. We propose a RoPE (Su et al., 2022) embedding scaling method that enables us to pretrain
099              language models from scratch at larger sequence length context size than the equivalent
100              GPU memory. We scaled the RoPE embedding through a shrinking factor by dividing the
101              target context length y by max_permissible_context_size_length on single GPU keeping
102              all other hyperparameters ﬁxed such as batch size, vocabulary size.
103           2. We propose a novel tokenization method using a combination of both Byte-Pair Encoding
104              (BPE) (Sennrich et al., 2016) and Unigram (Kudo, 2018) algorithms.
105
              3. We perform language speciﬁc tokenization for multilingual model and domain speciﬁc to-
106              kenization for monolingual language modeling.
107
         2
             https://www.babbel.com/en/magazine/the-10-most-spoken-languages-in-the-world


                                                          2
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


108
            4. We perform pretraining on comparable corpora for multilingual/bilingual generative lan-
109            guage model to handle data imbalance and curse-of-multi-linguality in multilingual lan-
110            guage model.
111
            5. We perform more than 1 epoch of pretraining for most our language models. From our
112
               results, we observed the language transfer from low resource to high resource within lan-
113            guage of the same script and typology. We found that small models trained for more than
114            1 epoch on high quality data is better than bigger model trained for 1 epoch on not so good
115            quality bigger corpora. We studied the impact of language speciﬁc tokenization versus lan-
116            guage agnostic tokenization for bilingual language modeling. We also studied the impact
117            of BPE vs Unigram tokenization for Devanagari script languages as a proof-of-concept.
118         6. We curated books for pretraining dataset and created an instruction-tuning dataset of 23000
119            instructions and instruction-tune our pretrained Bangla, Hindi, Marathi, Tamil, and Tel-
120            ugu models using 23000 instructions each in respective languages but for Hindi, we used
121            additional 52,000 Alpaca translated Hindi (Taori et al., 2023), total of 75,000 instructions.
122
123
      2     R ELATED W ORK
124
125
      The rise of large language models (LLMs) has led to numerous new models competing across var-
126   ious benchmarks, but most remain English-centric. Bloom, the largest multilingual LLM with 170
127   billion parameters, utilizes 40% English data and 60% from 44 other languages. Despite extensive
128   multilingual training, many LLMs exhibit a signiﬁcant English bias. Research has shown that a
129   substantial portion of neurons in these models remains inactive; for instance, in the 66 billion param-
130   eter OPT model, over 70% of the feed-forward network (FFN) neurons in certain layers are “dead”
131   meaning they do not activate even on diverse datasets. This sparsity in neuron activation limits the
132   model’s ability to learn and generalize across languages, particularly those that differ signiﬁcantly
133   from English (Voita et al., 2024). Consequently, even when trained on multilingual data, these mod-
134
      els struggle to effectively process and generate text in languages beyond English, revealing a critical
      limitation in their design. Recent efforts to adapt English-centric models like Llama for Indian
135
      languages (Airavata (Gala et al., 2024), OpenHathi for Hindi, and Tamil) have involved extending
136
      vocabulary and ﬁne-tuning techniques such as LoRA (Hu et al., 2022) and QLoRA (Dettmers et al.,
137   2023). However, these models still exhibit a strong English bias and struggle to generate high-quality
138   text in Indian languages. Massively multilingual models (MMTs) (Devlin et al., 2019), (Conneau
139   et al., 2020), (Xue et al., 2021) are pretrained on large corpora but often lack alignment between
140   languages, leading to poor transfer performance for distant languages (Lauscher et al., 2020). The
141   “curse of multilinguality” (CoM) indicates that adding more languages can degrade per-language
142   performance, necessitating larger model capacities and corpora. While language-speciﬁc adapters
143   like MAD-X (Pfeiffer et al., 2020) improve performance, they do not generalize well to unseen lan-
144   guages. Recent advancements, such as MAD-G (Ansell et al., 2021) and BAD-X (Parović et al.,
145
      2022), focus on bilingual adapters to enhance language transfer. Our work aims to create dedicated
      models for low-resource Indian languages by developing language-speciﬁc generative models from
146
      scratch, emphasizing linguistic features, typology, and tailored tokenization.
147
148
149   3     M ETHODOLOGY
150
151   3.1   DATASET FOR P RETRAINING
152
153
      Pretrained data was split into 95%-5% training and validation sets so that we do not lose much
      data for pretraining as the purpose of this work is a step towards developing pretrained generative
154
      effective language models from scratch using our novel architecture for Indian languages. Pretrain-
155
      ing data covers web scrapped news, blogs from IndicCorp v2 (Doddapaneni et al., 2023), Bangla
156   literature from Vacaspati (Bhattacharyya et al., 2023), Wikipedia articles, curated books of various
157   genres, subjects, education books, magazines in respective Indian languages representing each dis-
158   tinct language community to cover Indian culture, rich history, and knowledge. Our pretraining
159   corpora have no source code, scientiﬁc journals/articles, medical and engineering education books,
160   research papers as these are generally written in English in India. Dataset details can be found in
161   Table 19 in Appendix and data distribution can be found in the Figure 3 in Appendix. We apply the
      following data cleaning and preprocessing techniques as mentioned in Appendix A.4.

174   3.2    DATASET FOR I NSTRUCTION T UNING
175
176   We created a dataset of 5,000 human-annotated instructions covering tasks like poem and novel
177   writing, article summarization, grammar correction, and Q&A on topics such as climate change and
178   healthcare in Bangla, reﬂecting Bengali culture and linguistics. We then used the Google Translate
179   API (goo, 2023) to translate these instructions into Hindi, Marathi, Tamil, and Telugu. Additionally,
180   we translated 15,000 instructions from Dolly (Conover et al., 2023) to these languages and gener-
181
      ated 3,000 instructions using the self-instruct technique (Wang et al., 2023) in ﬁve Indian languages.
      Finally, we ﬁne-tuned our pretrained models except Hindi on a total of 23,000 instructions, includ-
182
      ing human-annotated, machine-generated, and translated instructions. Since creating multilingual
183
      datasets using automatic machine translation from Bengali to other Indian languages introduce trans-
184   lation errors but we also performed human checks and corrected them. We found around 8% word
185   errors on average for Hindi and Marathi using Google Translate.
186
187   3.3    T OKENIZATION
188
189   Figure 1 shows the ﬂowchart of our novel tokenization technique both for monolingual and mul-
190   tilingual settings. We performed domain adaptive tokenization for monolingual models using a
191   combination of Byte-Pair encoding (BPE) (Sennrich et al., 2016) and Unigram (Kudo, 2018). We
192   trained separate Byte-Pair encoding (BPE) (Sennrich et al., 2016) and Unigram tokenizers using
193   Sentencepiece (Kudo & Richardson, 2018) module on the high quality part of the pretraining data
194
      from scratch. Then, we merge both the independent tokenizers by intersection respectively of size
      V‘. This is similar to merging two different data structures of same size (considering each tokenizer
195
      as a list of tokens) by intersection to remove overlapping elements. We used the merged tokenizer
196
      to tokenize the pretraning data. During pre-tokenization, NFC normalization was performed on the
197   processed data; digits are split into individual tokens and unknown UTF-8 characters were reduced
198   to byte granularity. For monolingual tokenization, we trained individual BPE and Unigram tok-
199   enizers on domain speciﬁc data for respective language with same vocabulary size and merged the
200   tokenizers via merge by intersection to remove overlapping tokens and make our specialised tok-
201   enizer compact, optimized, effective and highly effective for monolingual data and also performed
202   the same approach to tokenize the multilingual pretraining corpora by performing language speciﬁc
203   tokenization in multi-task way where every task is a language and merged the distinct language
204   speciﬁc tokenizers by union via intersection, i.e, removing the overlapping tokens. In this way, our
205
      mBharat tokenizer was able to learn language speciﬁc tokens based on typology and can generalize
      to unseen languages of the same script. mBharat tokenizer was also exposed to little amount of
206
      English high quality corpus to learn its ability to tokenize languages of the Roman script such as En-
207
      glish. From 2, we observe that mBharat tokenizer has the least fertility score of 1.66 for languages in
208   Assamese-Bengali, 1.25 for Devanagari (Hindi) and 1.75 for Odia script among tokenizers of LLMs
209   like Sarvam 2B (Sarvam2B, 2024), LLama-3.1 (Dubey et al., 2024), Gemma-2 (Team et al., 2024),
210   and GPT-4o.
211
212   3.4    M ODEL A RCHITECTURE
213
214   Multilingual mParamanu, monolingual Paramanu models (Assamese, Bangla, Hindi, Konkani,
215   Marathi, Odia, Tamil, Telugu, and Paramanu-Sanskrit) and bilingual Konkani-Maithili models are
      based on transformer (Vaswani et al., 2017) based causal decoder architecture (Radford et al.,


                                                                     4
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


216
217
218
219
220
221
222
223
224
225
226               Figure 1: Tokenization technique for monolingual and multilingual setting.
227
228
229
230
231
232
233
234
235
236
237
238
239   Figure 2: Fertility score of mBharat tokenizer v/s LLMs across languages of 3 scripts (Bengali,
240   Devanagari, and Odia). LLMs score are reported from (Sarvam2B, 2024)
241
242   2019) with modiﬁcations. The model architecture uses RMSNorm as pre-normalizaion layer with
243   norm_epsilon = 1e-5, approximate GeGLU Activations (Shazeer, 2020) as non-linearity by replac-
244   ing the standard ReLU non-linearity activation function. The model architecture uses a scaled ver-
245   sion of RoPE embedding (Su et al., 2022) with θ=10,000. We scaled the RoPE embedding through
246   a shrinking factor by dividing the target context length y by permissible_context_size_length on
247   single GPU, keeping all other hyperparameters ﬁxed such as batch size, vocabulary size, etc. This
248   allows every position_ids to be divided by the shrinking ratio in the RoPE embedding methodol-
249   ogy. For instance, if the permissible_context_size_length for a given physical memory hardware
250
      is 256, then we apply shrinking factor of 16 for target context size of 4096 on Single A100 40G
      chip during pretraining. Then, a token with position_ids = 4000 becomes 4000/16 = 250, and the
251
      neighbouring token 4001 becomes 4001/16 = 250.06, to be within 0 to 256. This is how we can cap-
252
      ture higher context size during pretraining on limited physical memory required to pretrain model
253   at higher context size outside the permissible_context_size_length. This modiﬁcation allows us
254   to pretrain models from scratch at much higher context size than the physical memory required for
255   pretraining. Hence, with limited physical memory and limited GPUs, we can pretrain generative
256   language models from scratch at much higher desired context size. Following (Chowdhery et al.,
257   2023), we remove all biases from dense layers to improve the training stability. Table 17 lists down
258   the various conﬁguration to develop our 13.29M, 26.59M, 87M, 87.25M, 92.63M, 108.5M, 139.3M,
259   162M, 208M, 350M models. Table 17 lists the different model conﬁguration but we used weight
260   tying (Press & Wolf, 2017) to improve the performance of language models by sharing the weights
261
      of the embedding and softmax layers. Therefore, the total number of parameters in our models are
      typically smaller due to weight tying and different tokenizer sizes of each language speciﬁc model
262
      than the sizes mentioned in Table 17.
263
264
265   3.5   T RAINING
266
267   We performed hyperparameter tuning on 15M models to ﬁnd the optimal tokenizer size for each
268   language speciﬁc monolingual corpus, learning rate, learning rate scheduler, and weight decay. We
269   used the concept of µP transfer (Yang et al., 2021), and transferred the learned hyperparameters
      to our bigger models. For further training and hyperparameter tuning details, we mentioned in


                                                       5
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


270
      Appendix A.3. We ﬁnd the optimal size of tokenizers by training range of tokenizers of various sizes
271   and optimizing the validation perplexity of the language models and fertility score of the tokenizers.
272
273
      3.6   I MPACT OF LANGUAGE SPECIFIC MERGED - TOKENIZER V / S LANGUAGE AGNOSTIC
274
            TOKENIZER FOR B ILINGUAL MODEL (K ONKANI -M AITHILI 27M GPT) OF
275         TYPOLOGICALLY SAME SCRIPT (D EVANAGARI )
276
277   For the language agnostic approach, we merged both the Konkani and Maithili corpus which are of
278   comparable size after our preprocessing in terms of number of sentences (lines) then we trained Sen-
279   tencePiece’s BPE algorithm to a vocabulary size of 1750 on the whole corpus and trained our 27M
280   Konkani-Maithili bilingual GPT model and we report the validation perplexity score to be 12.43393.
281   For the language speciﬁc approach, we trained individual SentencePiece (Kudo & Richardson, 2018)
282   BPE tokenizers independently on Konkani (vocabulary size of 1k) and Maithili (vocabulary size of
283
      750) corpus respectively. After that, we merged both the language speciﬁc tokenizers and also re-
      moving the overlapped tokens as both Konkani and Maithili are written in Devanagari script. We
284
      used this merged tokenizer to train another 27M bilingual Maithili-Konkani GPT and found the vali-
285
      dation perplexity of the bilingual modeling drops to 8.53827 from 12.43393. From our experiments,
286   we infer that language speciﬁc tokenization is very important to preserve the language speciﬁc lin-
287   guistics even for thre typologically similar languages (Devanagari script) and the merging operation
288   of the tokenization also helps to omit the overlapping tokens which results in optimized vocabulary
289   size for language modeling resulting in better open-end text generation, lower perplexity score due
290   to optimal size of the tokenizer for multilingual language modeling.
291
292   3.7   L ANGUAGE M ODELING FOR PARAMANU -KONKANI (D EVANAGARI SCRIPT ) 15M WITH
293         BPE TOKENIZER V / S U NIGRAM TOKENIZER
294
295   From our experiments where we tokenized the Konkani corpus with Sentencepiece’s Unigram model
296   with a vocabulary size of 1000 and trained a 15 Model and similarly we also trained another 15M
297
      model with SentencePiece BPE tokenizer on the same setting to see whether there is an impact of
      these two different tokenizers on the perplexity score of monolingual language modeling. We found
298
      the validation perplexity for Unigram 15M model to be 11.88412 whereas for BPE 15M model is
299
      11.74314 which is slightly lower but not signiﬁcantly different. However, Unigram tokenizer takes
300   longer time than BPE to get trained on the corpus.
301
302
      3.8   Q UANTITATIVE R ESULTS BASED ON P ERPLEXITY M ETRIC
303
304   Table 2 lists the validation perplexity and MFU metrics of our various pretrained models. In terms
305   of quantitative evaluation of language modeling, the lower the perplexity, the better is the language
306   model. From Table 2, Paramanu-Sanskrit exhibited the lowest validation perplexity among our
307   models, followed by Odia, Bangla, Telugu, mParamanu (162M), Tamil, Marathi, and Hindi, which
308   had the highest perplexity. It is important to note that most models were trained for the same number
309   of steps, regardless of their pretraining dataset size, meaning some may be under-trained. Continued
310   pretraining, such as for Paramanu-Hindi, could further improve perplexity scores.
311
312   3.9   BASED ON Q UANTITATIVE B ENCHMARK
313
314   We evaluated our models on key Natural Language Understanding (NLU), Natural Language Infer-
315
      ence, and Commonsense Reasoning tasks, including their multilingual variants, while benchmarking
      against multilingual LLMs like Sarvam-2B, Bloom, and Bloomz in two groups, one with LLMs of
316
      size <=2B and another group of LLMs of size > 2B. We utilized the translated ARC, HellaSwag, and
317
      MMLU datasets from (Lai et al., 2023), employing Eleuther AI’s LM Evaluation Harness (Sutawika
318   et al., 2024) for evaluation. Key assessments included HellaSwag (Zellers et al., 2019), which tests
319   common sense reasoning by predicting scenario endings; MMLU (Hendrycks et al., 2021), mea-
320   suring broad knowledge across diverse subjects; and ARC-Challenge (Clark et al., 2018), which
321   examines complex reasoning with scientiﬁc questions. We encountered misalignment issues with
322   the LM Evaluation Harness datasets and our models, preventing evaluations on HellaSwag except
323   for Hindi. Other evaluations included XCOPA (Ponti et al., 2020), assessing cross-lingual common-
      sense reasoning; XNLI (Conneau et al., 2018), which assesses cross-lingual sentence classiﬁcation

346   model performance and reasoning capabilities.
347
      Table 4, Table 5, Table 6, Table 7 and Table 8 evaluate model performance in a zero-shot setting
348
      using accuracy metrics across translated benchmarks (ARC, MMLU, HellaSwag) for Bangla, Hindi,
349   Tamil, and Telugu. Table 3 presents n-shot evaluations for XNLI in Hindi, XStoryCloze in Hindi
350   and Telugu, and XCOPA in Tamil. Additionally, Table 5 and Table 6 assess various pretrained
351   monolingual and multilingual models for Devanagari script across MMLU, HellaSwag, ARC, XS-
352   toryCloze, XNLI, and Belebele, highlighting cross-lingual language transfer among Devanagari lan-
353   guages (Hindi, Marathi). Notably, mParamanu, pretrained on low-resource Devanagari languages
354   (Sanskrit, Konkani, Maithili), achieved scores of 25.86 for MMLU-Marathi, 24.84 for Hindi, 28 for
355   Belebele-Marathi, and 25.44 for Belebele-Hindi, indicating effective language transfer to medium
356   (Marathi) and high-resource (Hindi) languages using the mBharat tokenizer. Interestingly, neither
357
      Paramanu-Sanskrit nor mParamanu were pretrained on Hindi or Marathi but still performed well on
      their benchmarks. This is possibly due to the same script.
358
359   Table 5, Table 13 in Appendix and Table 6, Table 14 in Appendix show that Paramanu models
360   exhibit superior performance across various benchmarks in Devanagari languages, despite their
361   smaller sizes and being pretrained on fewer tokens than larger multilingual LLMs. Speciﬁcally,
362   Paramanu-Marathi (208M) outperformed Sarvam (2B), OpenHathi (7B), and Bloom (560M) on
363
      the Marathi benchmark. Similarly, mParamanu (162M) outperformed Paramanu-Hindi (367M) and
      demonstrated competitiveness against larger models. Notably, Paramanu-Hindi-instruct (356M) sur-
364
      passed all larger multilingual LLMs, except Bloomz (7B), by a signiﬁcant margin, beneﬁting from
365
      instruction tuning on a dataset of 27,000 Hindi instructions and additional 52,000 Alpaca machine-
366   translated instructions. In contrast, Bloomz (7B) was trained on hundreds of thousands of instruc-
367   tions. If the 367M Hindi model underwent more training steps, it could potentially achieve even
368   better performance, as many models were pretrained for the same duration regardless of dataset
369   size. The stronger performance of mParamanu in Hindi illustrates effective language transfer within
370   the same script and typology. Notably, Paramanu-Sanskrit (139M), pretrained on 45 billion tokens,
371   achieved an average score of 31.05, surpassing both Hindi models and closely approaching Bloom
372   (560M) and Bloomz (560M). Its lower perplexity (1.75) compared to Paramanu-Hindi (11.05) fur-
373   ther supports the notion that additional pretraining for the Hindi model could signiﬁcantly enhance
374
      its downstream performance.
375   From Table 3, we observe that the performance of our models drop from zero-shot setting to 25 shot
376   setting on XNLI-Hindi, XStoryCloze for Hindi and Telugu, and XCOPA for Tamil. This type of
377   phenomenon has also been observed in PlanningBench (Valmeekam et al., 2024) where GPT-3.5-
      Turbo, GPT-4, GPT4-o performance on Blocksworld dropped from 0 shot to 1-shot signiﬁcantly.

397   benchmarks report Accuracy except for ARC (Normalized Accuracy). Max scores are in bold.
398
399   Perhaps n-shot examples become additional soft constraints on the generation which might be the
400   reason of degradation of performance from the original training dataset. From Table 9, we see
401   that Paramanu-Bangla 108M outperformed Bloom 560M by 1.06% points, Bloomz 560M by 1.05%
402   points, Bloom 1.1B on average score across MMLU, ARC, and Belebele benchmarks, and by 1.21%
403   points on MMLU over Bloom 560M depsite being smaller by 10.2 times compared to Bloom 1.1B
404   and being pretrained on 26.21 billon of tokens. However, Paramanu-Bangla 108M is extensively
405
      trained only on Bangla literature corpus. With further instruction-tuning on 27k Bangla instructions,
      Paramanu-Bangla-instruct 108M outperformed Bloom 560M, Bloomz 560M, Bloom 1.1B, Sarvam
406
      2B, Bloom 7B on average score of MMLU, ARC, and Belebele benchmarks for Bangla respectively.
407
408   Table 7 and Table 8 compare pretrained multilingual LLMs (<2B) and instruction-tuned models on
409   Tamil and Telugu benchmarks. Table 15 and Table 16 in Appendix compares our model with LLMs
410   (> 2B) Our model, Paramanu-Tamil (208M), outperformed larger multilingual LLMs like Bloom
411
      (560M), Bloomz (560M), and Bloom (1.1B) across four benchmarks (Belebele, XCOPA, MMLU,
      and ARC) in Tamil, coming close to Sarvam (2B) despite being much smaller and trained on fewer to-
412
      kens. On MMLU-Tamil, both Paramanu-Tamil and Paramanu-Tamil-instruct outperformed Sarvam
413
      (2B) by 2.89 percentage points, with Paramanu-Tamil pretrained on 26.2 billion tokens. Paramanu-
414   Tamil-instruct surpassed Bloom (7B), despite being 34 times smaller, and outperformed Bloom
415   (1.1B) by 2.28 points, Bloom (560M) by 3 points, and Bloomz (560M) by 1.17 points. For Telugu,
416   Paramanu-Telugu-instruct (208M) outperformed Bloom (560M) by 2.72 points, Bloomz by 2.25
417   points, Bloom (1.1B) by 1.46 points, and Bloom (7B) by 1.22 points, with Paramanu-Telugu pre-
418   trained on 39.32 billion tokens. The improvements in metric scores for Tamil and Telugu instruction-
419   tuned models were modest, likely due to lower-quality machine translations from Bangla compared
420   to Hindi. Nonetheless, these results show strong performance of our models on various NLP tasks
421   despite their smaller size and fewer tokens, challenging the notion that larger models are always
422
      better. Our ﬁndings suggest that smaller pretrained models can excel when trained on high-quality,
      preprocessed data over multiple epochs, outperforming larger models trained on lower-quality data
423
      for an epoch.

428   multilingual Bloom series) alongside our pretrained models (Paramanu-Bangla, Paramanu-Hindi,
429   and mParamanu for Sanskrit) without ﬁne-tuning. The prompts reﬂected the local, cultural, and
430   literary contexts of Assamese, Bangla, Hindi, Konkani, Maithili, Odia, and Sanskrit. Due to resource
431   constraints, extensive evaluations focused on Paramanu-Bangla, Paramanu-Hindi, and mParamanu.
      The top three predictions from each model were generated with temperature = 1.0 and topp = 0.9.

449
450   Native speakers assessed the outputs on Grammar, Coherence, Creativity, and Factuality, each scored
451
      from 0 (worst) to 5 (best). For human evaluation, we asked 10 annotators to evaluate top-3 responses
      from models for each prompt on a scale of 0 (worst) to 5 (best). We report the average score of all
452
      ratings. We also have reported normalised scores of ratings in Table 19 in appendix to handle
453
      inconsistencies among annotators. We reached inter-annotator kappa score of 0.85 for Bengali, 0.79
454   for Hindi, and 0.72 for Sanskrit. Figure 4 in the Appendix shows the bar chart for inter-annotator
455   agreement’s Fleiss Kappa score. For Factuality, higher scores indicated better alignment with real
456   events, with some evaluators assigning a score of 0 when premises could not be veriﬁed.
457
      Table 24 displays the human evaluation of Paramanu-Bangla 87.25M model for the mentioned
458
      Bangla prompts. Paramanu-Bangla 87.25M model scored an average score of 3.5/5 on grammar,
459
      3.325/5 on coherence, 3.225/5 on creativity, and 3.2/5 on factuality metrics across top 3 generations
460   for each Bangla prompt. Table 10 compares the performance of Paramanu-Hindi 162M and other
461   LLMs including multilingual Bloom which was pretrained on Indian languages. In this table, we
462   can complete see that none of the open source LLMs have the ability to generate grammatically,
463   coherent sentences in Hindi except the Bloom series. Our monolingual model, Paramanu-Hindi
464   162M has performed better by 17.25% on grammar, by 46.05% on coherence, by 62.5% on cre-
465   ativity, and by 238.5% on factuality compared to Bloom 3B model despite being 19 times smaller
466   in size. Table 11 in Appendix compares the performance of mParamanu-162M and other LLMs
467   including multilingual Bloom which was pretrained on Indian languages. We can see that none
468
      of the LLMs have the ability to generate grammatically, coherent sentences in Sanskrit keeping
      aside the factuality. Our multilingual model, mParamanu-162M has scored the highest among all on
469
      grammar (3.75/5), coherence (3.166/5), creativity (2.166/5), and factuality (1.75/5) whereas Bloom
470
      3B scored 0.166/5 on grammar, 0.0833/5 on coherence, and 0/5 for both creativity and factuality
471   metrics respectively. GPT-3.5-Turbo (ChatGPT) has scored very poorly 0.25/5 on grammar & co-
472   herence metrics, 0.1818/5 in creativity and 0.33/5 on factuality metrics respectively for Sanskrit text
473   generation. mParamanu-162M is smaller by 44.25 times compared to 7B LLaMa-2 model and yet it
474   has shown its high quality text generation in Sanskrit as compared to ChatGPT, LLaMa, and Bloom
475   series of models.
476
477
      4      C ONCLUSIONS AND F UTURE W ORK
478
479
      In this paper, we present a series of novel efﬁcient small monolingual, bilingual, and multilingual
480   pretrained auto regressive models the range of 13.5M to 367.5M for 10 Indian languages across 5
481   scripts excluding English, thereby enabling GenAI NLP technology accessible to billion speakers in
482   the world and working towards including underrepresented languages to reduce the language bias
483   and divide in today’s NLP technology. We proposed a RoPE embedding scaling method that enables
484   us to pretrain language models from scratch at larger sequence length context without increased
485   GPU memory. We proposed novel tokenization technique of combining both BPE and Unigram to-
      kenizers into a single tokenizer. We also proposed and performed language speciﬁc tokenization for


                                                                              9
      Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025

504   Table 9: Human evaluation results (average scores of top-3 generations per prompt) of open-end text
505   generation of Paramanu-Bangla v/s LLMs for 4 Bangla prompts on Grammar, Coherency, Creativity,
506   and Factuality metrics. Scale is 0 (worst) to 5 (best). GPT-3.5-Turbo was accessed in October 2023.
507
508   multilingual language modeling and domain speciﬁc tokenization for monolingual language mod-
509   eling for strong performance. Our multilingual tokenizer, mBharat shows the best fertility scores
510
      among Indian language tokenizers. We considered typological grouping and pretraining on compa-
      rable size of each monolingual language corpus for our multilingual mParamanu 162M to handle
511
      data imbalance and curse of multilinguality. We evaluated our models for open-end text generation
512
      with human evaluators on grammar, coherency, creativity, and factual metrics. We reached inter-
513   annotator kappa score of 0.85 for Bengali, 0.79 for Hindi, and 0.72 for Sanskrit. In our evaluation,
514   we found that none of the popular existing LLMs can generate grammatically correct and coherent
515   sentences in 10 Indian languages despite being pretrained on Indian language corpora. Our efﬁcient
516   generative language models have performed better than Bloom 7B, LLaMa-2 7B, OPT 6.7B, GPT-
517   J 6B, GPTNeo 1.3B, GPT2-XL models for open-end text generation in Assamese, Bangla, Hindi,
518   Odia, and Sanskrit despite being 66 times to 20 times smaller in size. We also evaluated our models
519   and compared with several multilingual LLMs across various NLU, NLI, and commonsense reason-
520   ing benchmarks. Our models outperformed most multilingual LLMs of size 2B and performed very
521
      competitive or even better than LLMs of size 7B on various LLM benchmarks despite being smaller
      in size by by multiple order of magnitude compared with LLMs whose size is bigger by multiple
522
      order of magnitude. We observed language transfer phenomena from low-resource to high resource
523
      languages of same script and typology. We also instruction-tuned our pretrained models for Bangla,
524   Hindi, Marathi, Tamil, and Telugu and show their task handling capabilities.
525
526   In future, we would like to extend our multilingual model to 22 ofﬁcial Indian languages and align
527
      our generative language models with multimodal encoders to develop multimodal generative lan-
      guage models for Indian languages.
528

1053   A.3.1     26.58M PARAMANU -A SSAMESE MODEL
1054   We used the same training procedure as mentioned in 3.5 but with a batch size of 64, gradient
1055   accumulation steps of 4, and the maximum sequence length set to 1024, i.e., 262,144 tokens per
1056   iteration and transferred the learned hyperparameters from 15M model to 42M model using (µP)
1057   transfer. We set maximum learning rate (lr) to 3e-3 (max), weight decay to 1e-1. We trained our
1058   bigger models with fused AdamW optimizer for an epoch of training with β1 =0.9, β2 =0.95, dropout
1059   of 0.0, and scaled the gradient norms using a maximum norm clipping value of 1.0, and weight decay
1060   of 0.1. For our experiments and modeling, we implemented our code using Pytorch 2.0, in-house
1061   optmized CUDA kernels and used torch.compile feature for every model. To further speedup
1062
       training, we used BF16 mixed precision training.
1063

       A.3.2     87.25M PARAMANU -BANGLA MODEL

1066   accumulation steps of 8, and the maximum sequence length set to 1024, i.e., 262,144 tokens per
1067   iteration and transferred the learned hyperparameters from 15M model to 110M model using (µP )
1068   transfer.

1116   A.3.8        13.5M BILINGUAL KONKANI -M AITHILI GPT MODEL
1117
1118   Both Konkani and Maithili languages are typologically similar (Devanagari script). For bilingual
1119   model, we trained two 27M models with language agnostic tokenization and language speciﬁc tok-
1120
       enization to study the impact of language speciﬁc tokenization against language agnostic tokeniza-
       tion for multilingual language modeling. For training with the language speciﬁc tokenization, where
1121
       we basically trained independent BPE tokenizer on Konkani and Maithili corpora with a tokenizer
1122
       size of 1000 and 750 respectively and merged them together. For language agnostic tokenization,
1123   we trained BPE tokenizer on the merged corpora of Konkani and Maithili with a tokenizer size of
1124   1750.
1125
1126
       We used the same training procedure as mentioned in 3.5 but with a batch size of 128, gradient
       accumulation steps of 2, and the maximum sequence length set to 1024, i.e., 262,144 tokens per
1127
       iteration . We transferred the learned hyperparameters from 15M model to 27M model using (µP)
1128
       transfer.

1133   ing and having comparable corpora as shown in the Table 19, to avoid pretraining our multilingual
       model on disproportionate corpora of multiple languages. Thus, we avoid adding the Hindi corpus

       corpora as shown in Table 19.
1170
1171   We used the same training procedure as mentioned in 3.5 but with a batch size of 32, gradient
1172   accumulation steps of 8, maximum sequence length set to 1024, i.e., 524,288 tokens per iteration
1173   and a language speciﬁc multilingual tokenizer (mBharat).
1174
1175   A.3.10      162M MULTILINGUAL M PARAMANU MODEL
1176
       We used the same training procedure as mentioned in 3.5 but with a batch size of 32, gradient
1177
       accumulation steps of 8, the maximum sequence length set to 1024, i.e., 262,144 tokens per iteration.
1178
       We used language speciﬁc tokenization for the multilingual mBharat-162M model. It took 4 days
1179   and 11 hours to get trained on single A100-40G GPU with the mentioned speciﬁcations.

       A.5     H UMAN E VALUATION
1262
       A.5.1    M PARAMANU -162M VS B LOOM 1.1B E VALUATION
1263
1264   From Table 20 we see that Bloom 1.1B model could not distinguish languages of the same script so
1265   when we prompt Bloom with Konkani, Maithili, and Sanskrit (Devanagari script), Bloom only gener-
1266   ated incoherent, grammatically incorrect text in Hindi whereas our multilingual model, mParamanu-
1267   162M was able to recognise prompt in respective distinguished languages Konkani, Maithili, and
1268
       Sanskrit (Devanagari) to generate grammatically sound and coherent text in respective languages.
       Similarly, Bloom 1.1B could not identify Assamese (Assamese-Bengali Script) and generated out
1269
       of context Bangla words including series of Bangla alphabets for the Assamese prompt as shown
1270
       in the table 20 whereas mParamanu-162M model generated coherent sentences in Assamese when
1271   prompted with Assamese prompt. However, mParamanu-162M model is 7 times smaller in size than
1272   Bloom 1.1B and has better text generation capabilities in terms of grammar, coherence metrics for
1273   languages under consideration. This makes us to ascertain that despite Bloom being pretrained on 45
1274   languages including Indian languages yet its text generation capabilities is very limited in terms of
1275   grammatically correct and coherent text generation for Indian languages. Therefore, its not the right
1276   way to pretrain on many languages together varying script at the same time without considering
1277   linguistics typology, features, and disproportionate pretraining low quality corpus in multilingual
1278   setting.
1279
1280
       A.5.2    BANGLA E VALUATION
1281   We hard prompted the pretrained models with the following Bangla prompts, prompt 1: েফ-
1282   লুদা েতাপেসেক বলল, যা লালেমাহনবাবুেক খবর েদ! (phēludā tōpasēkē balala, yā
1283   lālamōhanabābukē khabara dē) – Feluda told Tapshe, Go and call Lalmohanbabu (from
1284   a popular detective novel series), prompt 2: এমন সময় হঠাৎ বােঘর ডাক! (ēmana samaẏa
1285   haṭhāt bāghēra ḍāka!) – suddenly there was a tiger roar (astonishing fearful expression),
1286   prompt 3: অপু এেস ডাকল, মা! (apu ēsē ḍākala, mā!) – Apu came and called, Mother (Apu is
1287   a character in a popular novel, also adapted into a popular movie), prompt 4: েস খুব খুশী হেলা। (sē
1288   khuba khuśī halō) – she became very happy (generic statement). All these prompts are related
1289   either to common expression in Bangla or covers diverse Bangla literature and cultural context in
1290   Bangla speaking community in West Bengal, India.
1291   Table 24 displays the human evaluation of Paramanu-Bangla 87.25M model for the mentioned
1292   Bangla prompts. We see Paramanu-Bangla 87.25M model scored an average score of 3.5/5 on
1293   grammar, 3.325/5 on coherence, 3.225/5 on creativity, and 3.2/5 on factuality metrics across top
1294   3 generations for each Bangla prompt. Table 9 compares the performance of Paramanu-Bangla
1295   108.5M and other LLMs including multilingual Bloom which was pretrained 45 languages includ-
       ing Indian languages. In this table, we can see that none of the open source LLMs have the ability

1569   ward pass and the entire calculation of the forward pass is kept in FP32. As we see that as we
1570   keep increasing the number of parameters in the model, the inference speed gets lower which is not
1571   preferable for deployment purpose due to larger model checkpoint ﬁles (4 bytes per every individual
1572   weight) and forward pass is relatively slow. One of the common inference optimization employed
1573   in practice is to quantize the model parameters to lower precision, while slightly giving up on pre-
1574
       cision (correctness) in return for smaller checkpoint sizes and faster forward passes (as most of the
       inference uses integer arithmetic). Table 25 shows the CPU inference speed of our models without

1593   C.0.1    L ANGUAGE M ODELING
1594
1595   This objective of the language modeling can be formally described as maximizing the probability of
1596
       a sequence of tokens w1 , w2 , . . . , wN
1597                                                         ∏
                                                             n

1598                           P (w1 , w2 , . . . , wn ) =         P (wi | w1 , w2 , . . . , wi−1 )
1599                                                         i=1

1600   where p(wt |w0 , . . . wt−1 ) is the probability of token wt given the sequence of previous tokens
1601   w0 , . . . , wt−1 .
1602
       The performance of a language model is generally evaluated using the total cross-entropy loss, i.e,
1603   the negative log-likelihood of the observed data under the model under consideration, which for a
1604   given dataset is deﬁned as:
1605
                                              1 ∑
                                                   n
1606                             Loss = −           log(P (wi | w1 , w2 , . . . , wi−1 ))
1607                                          N i=1
1608
       Lower the loss better is the model; however, just computing the loss may not be intuitive. Therefore,
1609
       Perplexity is a metric to evaluate the performance of a given language model which is the exponent
1610   of the average loss.
1611
1612                                          P erplexity = exp (Loss)
1613
       C.0.2    F ERTILITY S CORE OF T OKENIZERS
1614
1615   The fertility score is a key metric used to evaluate the performance of tokenizers in natural language
1616   processing (NLP). It quantiﬁes how well a tokenizer divides a given text into meaningful tokens
1617   while preserving the linguistic structure and semantic content of the original input. Speciﬁcally,
1618   the fertility score is deﬁned as the ratio of the number of tokens produced by the tokenizer to the
1619   expected number of tokens that would be produced by an idealized, reference tokenization. Mathe-
       matically, it is expressed as:


                                                              30
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1620
1621                                                     Ntokens
1622                                               F =
                                                         Nexpected
1623
1624   Where Ntokens is the number of tokens generated by the tokenizer, and Nexpected is the number of
1625   tokens expected in an ideal scenario, often determined through linguistic analysis or human anno-
1626   tations. A fertility score close to 1 indicates that the tokenizer is performing optimally, generat-
1627   ing a number of tokens that aligns with the reference standard. A score greater than 1 suggests
1628   over-segmentation, where the tokenizer generates more tokens than necessary, possibly losing some
1629   meaning or context in the process. A score below 1 indicates under-segmentation, where the tok-
1630   enizer groups multiple linguistic units into fewer tokens, potentially missing out on ﬁner details.
1631   Several factors can affect the fertility score of a tokenizer, including the granularity of tokenization
1632   (e.g., character-level vs. word-level), vocabulary coverage (whether the tokenizer can match entire
1633   words or subword units), and how special characters and punctuation are handled. Moreover, the
1634   characteristics of the language being processedsuch as morphological complexitycan also inﬂuence
1635   tokenization, especially in languages with rich inﬂections or compounds.
1636   The fertility score is important for ensuring that tokenization strikes a balance between semantic
1637   precision and computational efﬁciency. In tasks like machine translation, where retaining meaning
1638   is crucial, a higher fertility score may be desired to preserve linguistic nuances. However, in real-
1639   time applications where speed is critical, a lower fertility score may be preferred to reduce the
1640   number of tokens and computational load.
1641   For example, consider the sentence “I love natural language processing.” A tokenizer that produces
1642   the following tokens: [I, love, natural, language, processing] would have a fer-
1643   tility score of:
1644
1645                                                      5
1646                                                F =     =1
                                                          5
1647
1648   This score suggests that the tokenizer is performing as expected. However, if another tokenizer splits
1649   “natura” into “natur” and “al,” producing the tokens [I, love, natur, al, language,
1650   processing], the fertility score would be:
1651
1652                                                      6
1653
                                                   F =      = 1.2
                                                          5
1654
1655   This indicates over-segmentation, with more tokens than ideal.
1656   In summary, the fertility score of a tokenizer is an essential metric that helps to assess the efﬁciency
1657   and effectiveness of tokenization strategies. By optimizing the fertility score, one can ensure that
1658   tokenization maintains the right balance between computational efﬁciency and the preservation of
1659   meaning, making it a crucial aspect of NLP systems.
1660
1661   C.0.3    ROTARY P OSITION E MBEDDING (RO PE)
1662
1663   Transformer-based models rely on positional embeddings to encode position and relative location
1664   information of words in a text. Rotary Position Embedding (RoPE) is a position encoding technique
1665
       proposed by (Black et al., 2022). Instead of adding positional embeddings or relative positional
       embeddings to token embeddings, RoPE rotates the token embedding by a ﬁxed factor (θ) in the
1666
       higher-dimensional space to encode relative positional embeddings. In other words, RoPE encodes
1667
       the absolute positions with a rotation matrix and meanwhile incorporates the explicit relative position
1668   dependency in self-attention formulation. The intuition behind RoPE is that we can represent the
1669   token embeddings as complex numbers and their positions as pure rotations that we apply to them.
1670   If we shift both the query and key by the same amount, changing absolute position but not relative
1671   position, this will lead both representations to be additionally rotated in the same manner. Thus, the
1672   angle between them will remain unchanged and, thus, the dot product will also remain unchanged.
1673   By exploiting the nature of rotations, the dot product used in self-attention will have the property for
       preserving relative positional information while discarding absolute position.


                                                          31
       Under review as a conference paper at ICLR 2025


1674
       C.0.4    ROOT M EAN S QUARE N ORMALIZATION (RMSN ORM )
1675
1676   To improve the training stability, some LLMs (Chinchilla (Hoffmann et al., 2022a), LLaMa (Tou-
1677   vron et al., 2023)) have normalized the input of each transformer sub-layer, instead of normalizing
1678   the output using RMSNorm normalizing function as introduced by (?). RMSNorm normalizes the
1679   activations based on their root mean square (RMS) value instead of normalizing the inputs based on
1680
       their mean and variance.
1681   RMSNorm accelerates the training and inference with similar performance in these large models.
1682   It is reported that replacing LayerNorm (Ba et al., 2016) with RMSNorm can achieve comparable
1683   performance and improve training and inference time by 7-64%. Narang et al. (2021) showed that
1684   RMSNorm improves the pre-training speed by 5% compared with the LayerNorm baseline.
1685
1686   C.1     M ODEL FLOP S U TILIZATION (MFU)
1687
1688
       Model FLOPs Utilization (MFU) Chowdhery et al. (2023) estimate is the ratio of the observed
       throughput (tokens-per-second) relative to the theoretical maximum throughput of a system at peak
1689
       FLOPs. Model ﬂops utilization (MFU) estimate the number of ﬂops (ﬂoating point operations) done
1690
       per iteration. It quantiﬁes how efﬁciently the GPUs are utilized in model training.
1691
1692
       C.2     M AXIMAL U PDATE PARAMETERIZATION
1693
1694   As the size of large language models (LLMs) and the scale of the dataset used in pretraining are ex-
1695   pensively large, it is not feasible to perform hyperparameter tuning in LLMs. Yang et al. (2021) used
1696   a technique called maximal update parameterization (µP ) to transfer the hyperparameters learnt
1697   from tuning of a small model to a larger model and found that the optimal hyperparameter values
1698   become stable across neural network sizes when the models have been parameterized using (µP ).
1699
1700   C.3     C ARBON F OOTPRINT
1701
1702
       To measure carbon footprint for our pretraining, we follow Touvron et al. (2023):
1703                                       tCO2 eq = M W h × 0.385
1704
1705
       The power consumption can be calculated as
1706                        W h = GPU-hours × (GPU power consumption) × PUE
1707
1708
       where PUE is Power Usage Effectiveness.
1709   We observed during pretraining that our single A100 40G consumes 250 Watt consistently.

2849   महात्मा गांधी   महात्मा गांधी भी अपनी जदगी में मयार्दा     महात्मा गांधी जयंती समारोह में भाग लेने        महात्मा गांधी को हराते हुए पा कस्तान ने        महात्मा गांधी क हत्या से लेकर 1947 से       महात्मा गांधी जी को श्रद्धांज ल दे ने के
                       का पालन करते थे और अगर वे हसा का           के लए तैयार है।                                भी इसे स्वीकार नहीं कया।                       1947 के बाद भारत क आजाद तक का                 लए, एक सप्ताह से भी कम समय बचा है,
